From heinlein at madboa.com  Fri Feb 11 05:34:05 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Fri, 11 Feb 2005 05:34:05 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Shell scripting and FTP
In-Reply-To: <9e0a357805021018163f34abe5@mail.gmail.com>
References: <9e0a357805021018163f34abe5@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0502110523580.4399@pecos.madboa.com>

On Thu, 10 Feb 2005, Matt Alexander wrote:

> I'd like to FTP all the files in a directory except one.  It would be
> nice if I could do this:
>
> ------------------
> #!/bin/sh
>
> cd files
> export FOO=`ls | sed 's/ignorefile.txt//'`
>
> /usr/bin/ftp -i -n <<EOF
> open ftp.someserver.com
> user myuser mypass
> mput $FOO
> bye
> EOF

If you have or can install the Net::FTP module from CPAN, then it's a 
simple perl script. The following is off the top of my head and hasn't 
been tested (so it may contain bone-head errors :-):

   use Net::FTP;

   opendir( FTPFILES, '.' ) || or die $!;
   my @tosend = grep { ! /^\./ or ! /^ignore/ } readdir( FTPFILES );
   closedir( FTPFILES ) or die $!;

   $ftp = Net::FTP->new("ftp.someserver", Debug => 0);
   $ftp->login("myuser",'mypass');
   foreach $tosend ( @tosend ) {
     $ftp->put( "$tosend", "/remote/dir/$tosend" );
   }
   $ftp->quit;

-- Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

point of having several programs open, close all but xterm, K3b still hangs 
at the same place, but I still can get to the xterm. I've tried killing the 
process ps ax shows for K3b, but nothing hapens. <ctrl><alt><bs> restarts X, 
but K3b still hangs while looking for the CD drive (the place it always 
hangs). The only way I've been able to get it to run is to reboot the machine 
and start K3b right away.

Knowing that this is not normal behavior for a Linux environment, what should 
I be looking for to enable me to reset whatever needs resetting so I can run 
K3b without a reboot?

I'm running SuSE 9.1 personal, which has the 2.6 kernel. (What else do I need 
to provide to help narrow down this problem?)

From rich at paranoid.org  Fri Feb 11 09:50:14 2005
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Fri, 11 Feb 2005 09:50:14 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] tinydns problems... help?
In-Reply-To: <420C24E3.1000605@servuhome.net>
References: <420C1F7C.4080406@hotpop.com>	<1108091735.7851.51.camel@dell.linux.box>
	<420C24E3.1000605@servuhome.net>
Message-ID: <420CF056.3080907@paranoid.org>

Brent Jones wrote:

> I will have to second this. DJB puts out a decent alternative, but BIND 
> is juat as secure, and in my experience, easier to use.

I think there are things you can do to run BIND more securely, and those 
have thankfully become more standard practice, but that's not the same 
as saying it's "just as secure." There have been quite a few holes in 
BIND in the pasr that led to root compromises. Now, you don't have to 
run it as root, and you can run it chrooted, and those things help 
greatly limit the damage that can be done. But there were some big 
problems in the code in the past, and I would argue that's not "just as 
secure."

> The reason a lot of 
> DJB's "bounties" have never been collected is because there isn't a huge 
> interest in compromising a seldomly used program for a measly $500 or 
> $1,000.

Yeah, I don't buy that either :) I can't believe that there are not some 
skilled people out there with black hats on who have gone through that 
code very throroughly. It wouldn't be just about the money - someone 
could make a bit of a name for themselves by collecting a bounty on one 
of Dan's programs, which are usually regarded as very secure.


Rich

From rich at paranoid.org  Fri Feb 11 14:24:04 2005
From: rich at paranoid.org (Rich Burroughs)
Date: Fri, 11 Feb 2005 14:24:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: tinydns problems... help?
In-Reply-To: <slrnd0pvju.31g.wcooley@rheingold.nakedape.priv>
References: <420C1F7C.4080406@hotpop.com>	<1108091735.7851.51.camel@dell.linux.box>	<420C24E3.1000605@servuhome.net>
	<420CF056.3080907@paranoid.org>
	<slrnd0pvju.31g.wcooley@rheingold.nakedape.priv>
Message-ID: <420D3084.7060304@paranoid.org>

Wil Cooley wrote:
> 
> But BIND 9 was total rewrite by someone skilled in writing secure C, which Paul
> Vixie readily claims he is incapable of.  Have there been any root exploits for
> BIND 9?  I don't know; there have been a few non-root exploits, but not many.

Perhaps the BIND code is much better now, Wil, I'm not sure. I guess I 
have a lot of bad memories from the old days. I remember one exploit 
that was used to r00t what seemed like half the DNS servers on the Net 
:) I'm pretty sure the last big one I can remember was BIND 4, though.

Still, it is hard for me to believe that even vresion 9 is just as 
secure code wise as tinydns, which is more stripped-down and was written 
with security as a big priority. I'm not saying I won't use BIND. I 
think that when you run it as a non-root user and chrooted, that it's 
not a huge risk. But I also encourage people to try other alternatives 
if they want.

I would be very surprised if there hadn't been less security problems 
with tinydns than even with BIND 9 in the same time perios, and I really 
disagree with that characterization that was made that Dan's tools are 
not ready for production. I'm not a DJB disciple - I would rather use 
Postfix than Qmail any day. But I've seen tinydns run in production and 
it worked great.


Rich

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Fri Feb 11 15:14:07 2005
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Fri, 11 Feb 2005 23:14:07 +0000 (UTC)
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Multiple PHP's
References: <9e0a357805021114132a7c26d@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <slrnd0qf2f.31g.wcooley@rheingold.nakedape.priv>

On 2005-02-11, Matt Alexander <lowbassman at gmail.com> wrote:
> I have a server running PHP 5.  PHP 5 has to stay.  However, I also
> need to install phpBB which doesn't work with PHP 5.  Is there a way
> to use two versions of PHP with Apache?  Maybe with a directory
> directive that only runs PHP 4 for the phpBB directory.  Or...?

Well, you can always run multiple Apache versions, each binding to different
IP addresses.  On the machine running the PLUG site, I have 1.3 running with
mod_perl (because Karl made the site with HTML::Mason and for the Slash-based
sites I run, like http://www.news4neighbors.net) and the CentOS-stock 2.0 for
the regular sites.  I run Pound (http://www.apsis.ch/pound/) on the firewall
to direct incoming HTTP requests to the proper address.  Being able to match
the Host HTTP header and forward to the proper IP address is much easier
than using mod_proxy or Squid and having to fiddle with /etc/hosts to keep
things straight.

Wil

> On Thursday 10 February 2005 10:57 am, Mike De La Mater wrote:
> > I'm looking for alternatives to traditional, burdensome, antivirus
> > software. Does anyone have experience with AVG at Grisoft .com?
> 
> For Windows, AVG can't be beat.  For Linux, ClamAV is a better pick.

F-Secure for Linux catches more viruses than ClamAV.  (Especially
trojans.)  Getting it is more of a pain than it should be.

ClamAV also has problems with false positives on multi-part rar and zip
files.  (Claims they are "oversized compressed files". I have had to
disable that check on scans.)

From aarontc at gmail.com  Fri Feb 11 14:52:36 2005
From: aarontc at gmail.com (Aaron Ten Clay)
Date: Fri, 11 Feb 2005 14:52:36 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Multiple PHP's
In-Reply-To: <9e0a357805021114132a7c26d@mail.gmail.com>
References: <9e0a357805021114132a7c26d@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <41f575d105021114523e6af6d@mail.gmail.com>

On Fri, 11 Feb 2005 15:13:30 -0700, Matt Alexander <lowbassman at gmail.com> wrote:
> I have a server running PHP 5.  PHP 5 has to stay.  However, I also
> need to install phpBB which doesn't work with PHP 5.  Is there a way
> to use two versions of PHP with Apache?  Maybe with a directory
> directive that only runs PHP 4 for the phpBB directory.  Or...?
> Thanks,
> ~M

I believe you can, assuming PHP is running as modules, have two directives like:

AddHandler php5-script php5
AddHandler php-script php4

Where php5 and php4 are the extensions of your files. Adjust
accordingly. Also note you may need an AddType text/html phpX
depending on your configuration.

Aaron

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Feb 12 07:39:43 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 12 Feb 2005 07:39:43 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Failing swap cage
Message-ID: <20050212153943.GA8335@gate.kl-ic.com>


In the last few days, I started seeing this error in the log files
of my backup server:

Feb 10 04:13:16 hdg: dma_intr: status=0x51 { DriveReady SeekComplete Error } 
Feb 10 04:13:16 hdg: dma_intr: error=0x84 { DriveStatusError BadCRC } 

hdg is the backup drive.   I got about 200 of these a night, during
backups of course.

I swap my backup drives every day, though I stopped when I saw this 
error, so I would not risk my other backup drives until I tracked
this down.  Fortunately, there was no apparent damage to the data
on the drive.

The error stopped when I replaced the swap cage hardware.  So, either
the electrical contact pins in the cage have worn out, the fan on the
swap has stopped, or the IDE connector in the back of the cage was
partly loose.  This kind of thing can happen with poor electrical
contact on the IDE connector - the signals become intermittent. 

And that can happen when the cage is being slammed with a new drive
removal/insertion every day - connectors can lose their gold plating,
or IDE cables can work their way loose.  There is a lot of weight
hanging on the IDE plug, and the plug and socket get flexed every
time the cage knocked about.

When I get time, I will narrow the cause down further, but for now,
if you see this kind of problem, check your IDE cable connections.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From pem at nellump.net  Sat Feb 12 09:36:17 2005

On Sat, Feb 12, 2005 at 08:24:08AM -0800, AthlonRob wrote:
> On Sat, 2005-02-12 at 07:39 -0800, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> > Feb 10 04:13:16 hdg: dma_intr: status=0x51 { DriveReady SeekComplete Error } 
> > Feb 10 04:13:16 hdg: dma_intr: error=0x84 { DriveStatusError BadCRC } 
> 
> Interesting you narrowed your issues down to a bad connector.  On my
> very old closet server, I receive identical errors in batches when the
> drive is in DMA mode.  With 2.4, it didn't work in DMA at all (enabling
> DMA would cause the drive to become completely nonresponsive).  With
> 2.6.9, however, DMA works!  It just pops up slews of those errors.

Make sure you're using an 80-wire cable (an extra ground conductor for
each signal conductor--reduces errors due to noise, etc.). I installed
an ATA/66 PCI card in an old PowerMac I use as my home server (yes,
Linux). I couldn't even get an IDE drive to work more than a few
minutes until I realized I was using a 40-wire cable (unsuitable for
anything more than ATA/33, IIRC). I still get a fair amount of the
exact same errors Keith is seeing, but they don't seem to cause any
problems. I keep the majority of the drive's partitions mounted
read-only, anyhow (for whatever that's worth).


Paul

out messages with (forged) cadtocam.com From: headers, which leads to 
b) remote sites bouncing those messages (erroneously) back to your 
server.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Feb 12 16:46:14 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 12 Feb 2005 16:46:14 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Failing swap cage 
In-Reply-To: <20050212202212.62B0B4250@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20050212202212.62B0B4250@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <20050213004614.GB11521@gate.kl-ic.com>


Keith wrote:
> ...
> Feb 10 04:13:16 hdg: dma_intr: status=0x51 { DriveReady SeekComplete Error } 
> Feb 10 04:13:16 hdg: dma_intr: error=0x84 { DriveStatusError BadCRC } 
...
> I swap my backup drives every day, though I stopped when I saw this 
...
> The error stopped when I replaced the swap cage hardware.  
...

Pete Lancashire asks:
> How many times have you swapped drives ?

Nightly for 1.5 years.  I do disk-to-disk backups, and rotate the
backups to a fire resistant safe.  This means there is always a fairly
recent drive available ("air-gap protected") in case of small fires,
power supply failures, or enemy action.  So that is around 500 cycles
of insertion/uninsertion.   BTW, I saw the errors for two different
drives.  

Besides the nightly drive swap, I have changed relatively little for
a long time.  So it wasn't new software, hardware changes, or proton
decay.  Other theories were an excessively large backup transfer,
bit rot in the in-RAM image, and a failing IDE controller card.  I
run a separate card - faster, and I don't lose the mobo if static
takes out the IDE connection during swapping.  If a reboot had worked
(it didn't) and the hardware swap did not, I would work on the other
hypotheses.  

Athlon Rob writes:
> Interesting you narrowed your issues down to a bad connector.  On my
> very old closet server, I receive identical errors in batches when the
> drive is in DMA mode.  With 2.4, it didn't work in DMA at all (enabling
> DMA would cause the drive to become completely nonresponsive).  With
> 2.6.9, however, DMA works!  It just pops up slews of those errors.
> 
> I don't get the errors with DMA disabled.
> 
> I suppose with less data flowing with DMA disabled, the errors might
> just be harder to trigger... but it does seem interesting.  I'll have to
> swap out the IDE cables the next time the box goes down for maintenance.

Think of electrical connections as piano strings.  Bang on them with a
signal transition ( 1 to 0, 0 to 1 ) and they will vibrate for a while.
If you space out the notes, then the ringing will go away and not get
added to the next transition (possibly making it difficult to detect).
So faster signals can cause more errors.

The extra ground wires in 80-wire IDE cables help damp the ringing. 
This is difficult to describe with easy analogies, but the idea is
that electrical signals always flow in loops, and loops both transmit
wanted energy and receive unwanted energy.  The bigger the loop, the
more signal energy is lost, and the more extraneous noise energy is
picked up.  If you put a ground (signal return) wire right next to
your signal wire, it makes the loop small, decreasing losses and
decreasing noise pickup.  And this minimizes ringing - sorta like
putting a small lead pipe around a piano string.

When I replaced the swap cage, I pulled the IDE cable off the back,
and the plug came off the cage just a tiny bit too easily.  I 
wanted good backups that night, and wanted to make the fix happen,
but my boss\\\\wife wanted me elsewhere, so I did not have time
to do a thorough fault isolation.  I was pleased to see no errors
in the log this morning;  hence the posting to PLUG.

Now it is time to do another drive swap, and make sure the fix stays.

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From kens at cad2cam.com  Mon Feb 14 06:51:19 2005
From: kens at cad2cam.com (Kenneth G. Stephens)
Date: Mon, 14 Feb 2005 06:51:19 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Perl DBI question
In-Reply-To: <9e0a3578050213165072f745f3@mail.gmail.com>
References: <9e0a3578050213165072f745f3@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <1108392679.615.88.camel@atlas.cadtocam.com>

On Sun, 2005-02-13 at 17:50 -0700, Matt Alexander wrote:
> I'm trying to use the "?" DBI syntax with a like statement and
> wildcards.  Here's what I want to do:
> 
> $sth = $dbh->prepare("select * from table where col1 like '%?%'");
> $sth->execute($value);
> 
> I've tried several different variations of where to put quotes, but
> nothing's worked so far.
> Any suggestions?
> Thanks,
> ~M
> 
Use the '\' escape character so that the '%'s are interpreted literally.

$sth - $dbh->prepare("select \* from table where col1 like \%\?\%");
-- 
Ken Stephens
CAD 2 CAM



From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Mon Feb 14 08:54:32 2005
From: merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz)
Date: 14 Feb 2005 08:54:32 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Perl DBI question
References: <9e0a3578050213165072f745f3@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <86650vdquv.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>

>>>>> "Matt" == Matt Alexander <lowbassman at gmail.com> writes:

Matt> I'm trying to use the "?" DBI syntax with a like statement and
Matt> wildcards.  Here's what I want to do:

Matt> $sth = $dbh->prepare("select * from table where col1 like '%?%'");
Matt> $sth->execute($value);

"?" must be an entire value... so you need something like this:

$sth = $dbh->prepare("select * from table where col1 like ?");
$sth->execute("%$value%");

Turned out the system I set up on the Pentium 4 didn't work with it.
Doing a minimal install on the K6-2 directly using a junk hard drive
and borrowing a cdrom, that system is nfs exproted from a different
machine now.  After slight modification, it works just fine.  
Is there a way to install Redhat 7.3 or any newer Redhat on a 
Pentium 4 in such a way that it will work on a VA503+?  My thought 
is that the i686 packages were picked by the Pentium 4.  NFS root 
is a lot easier if you have multiple computers of the exact same 
type.

Getting the wireless card working is another issue.  I tried compiling
the Linux driver for my Zonet Zew1300b on the host system, but when I
attempt to use it on my nfs booted system the driver seg faults and resists
rmmod commands.  I guess I'll have to try and compile it directly on
the K6-2 system.  I can use chroot to install software from underneath.
If I can get this wireless card to go as well, I'll replace the ne2000
when it arrives with an fa311 and be good to go.  My purpose is to build
a netbooted wireless router for mail and a Net backup.  I don't know if
I'll need to install postfix and there's also the question of how to 
get the wireless connection to encrypt.

I wish Redhat had an install root to NFS exported filesystem option, 
that would make doing this a lot easier.  I had to disable all the
consoles in inittab, devfs didn't create tty[1-6].  How would I get
a console if I wanted one?

All of the Howtos I found are missing, "these are the modifications you 
need to do to Redhat 7.3."  Redhat screwed up with their rc.sysinit,
it is almost nfs root ready but not quite.  I probably should remove
run levels 0 and 6 considering that they don't work right.

I often have to edit files on remote boxes.  I can ssh into all of these boxes,
but, for various reasons, I can't tunnel X on some of them.  So I use emacs
in console mode.

The problem is that some key combinations that my fingers are used to making
(like Ctrl-Home or Ctrl-Left) won't work in non-windowed emacs.

If I type the following keys in xev, I get:

Home:       state 0x0, keycode 97 (keysym 0xff50, Home)
Ctrl-Home:  state 0x4, keycode 97 (keysym 0xff50, Home)
Alt-Home:   state 0x8, keycode 97 (keysym 0xff50, Home)

The same keys typed into showkey -a in an xterm yield:

^[[1~    27 0033 0x1b
         91 0133 0x5b
         49 0061 0x31
        126 0176 0x7e
^[[1~    27 0033 0x1b
         91 0133 0x5b
         49 0061 0x31
        126 0176 0x7e
^[[1~    27 0033 0x1b
         91 0133 0x5b
         49 0061 0x31
        126 0176 0x7e

So what does this mean?  Is xterm just ignoring the state for keypad keys?
Or is he using some translation table to fold all of the Homes in together?

It's obvious that nothing I can put in my .emacs will allow it to differentiate
between these identical codes.  What should I be looking at instead?  xmodmap?
terminfo?  Something entirely different?

Ali

From lowbassman at gmail.com  Mon Feb 14 15:36:26 2005
From: lowbassman at gmail.com (Matt Alexander)
Date: Mon, 14 Feb 2005 16:36:26 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Perl DBI question
In-Reply-To: <86650vdquv.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
References: <9e0a3578050213165072f745f3@mail.gmail.com>
	<86650vdquv.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
Message-ID: <9e0a357805021415366a7771f3@mail.gmail.com>

On Mon, 14 Feb 2005 09:12:03 -0800 (PST), Randal L. Schwartz
<merlyn at stonehenge.com> wrote:
> >>>>> "Matt" == Matt Alexander <lowbassman at gmail.com > writes:
> 
> Matt> I'm trying to use the "?" DBI syntax with a like statement and
> Matt> wildcards.  Here's what I want to do:
> 
> Matt> $sth = $dbh->prepare("select * from table where col1 like '%?%'");
> Matt> $sth->execute($value);
> 
> "?" must be an entire value... so you need something like this:
> 
> $sth = $dbh->prepare("select * from table where col1 like ?");
> $sth->execute("%$value%");
> 

That's it!  Thanks!
~M

On Sun, 2005-02-13 at 17:50 -0700, Matt Alexander wrote:
> Any suggestions?

Yes, build your query as a string and then execute it.  :-)

$sql  = "SELECT * FROM table WHERE coll LIKE\'\%";
$sql .= $value . "\%\'";
$sth = $dbh->prepare($sql);
$sth->execute();

Just a matter of style, I think, but I prefer building SQL queries as
strings before executing them rather than using the "?" stuff.  IMHO, it
is simpler and easier to deal with and debug.

From jeme at brelin.net  Tue Feb 15 01:14:49 2005
From: jeme at brelin.net (Jeme A Brelin)
Date: Tue, 15 Feb 2005 01:14:49 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Perl DBI question
In-Reply-To: <1108452334.5309.10.camel@dagon.fnordora.org>
References: <9e0a3578050213165072f745f3@mail.gmail.com>
	<1108429550.8141.19.camel@dell.linux.box>
	<1108452334.5309.10.camel@dagon.fnordora.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0502150110400.2963@shaft.bitmine.net>


On Mon, 14 Feb 2005, Alan wrote:
> If the user has access to what gets fed into $value he can do things
> like.
>
> $value = 'something useless%";DROP table';

That's why every perl script with user input must begin with at least

#!/usr/bin/perl -wT
use strict;

Also, don't let scripts that do routine things connect to databases as 
users that have permission to do non-routine things.  If possible, narrow 
the permissions of the database user to exactly the operations the script 
will perform.  It's a pain to create a unique user for each script, sure, 
but at least then you know who's doing what and you don't have to edit all 
of your scripts when one is compromised.

Maybe there's a better way.

J.

From david.fleck at mchsi.com  Tue Feb 15 05:08:43 2005
From: david.fleck at mchsi.com (David Fleck)
Date: Tue, 15 Feb 2005 07:08:43 -0600 (CST)
Subject: [PLUG] Home wireless network dropouts
Message-ID: <20050215063534.Y94432@grond.sourballs.org>

We've got a small network set up in our house.  The cable modem and router 
are in the basement; two machines are connected to the router by cat5, and 
two have USB wireless adapters.  One wireless machine is in a 2nd-floor 
bedroom directly above the basement router; the other is in another 
2nd-floor bedroom, but at the opposite end of the house.

Connection between the router and the wireless machines is generally good, 
but the machine located farthest from the router (as a wild guess, I'll 
say 35 - 40 feet straight line distance) experiences periods of signal 
dropout.  Connectivity will disappear for 5 minutes, then be ok for 30 
minutes, then drop out for a few more minutes, then be ok for an hour, 
etc.

I'd like to fix this situation, if possible.  I don't think the router can 
be moved, but I wonder if another router might have greater signal 
strength, and if that might solve the problem.  The current router is a 
Netgear MR814v2 - we used to have Linksys but gave up on them because of a 
strange, difficult-to-track-down incompatibility with FreeBSD 
(apparently).

Not really relevant to the situation, but the 2 basement machines are 
FreeBSD, one wireless machine is SuSE 9.1, and the one suffering the 
dropout problem is a Debian (well, a Knoppix 3.3).

Any advice or information would be appreciated.

Thanks-

--
David Fleck
david.fleck at mchsi.com

> periods of signal dropout. Connectivity will disappear for 5 
> minutes, then be ok for 30 minutes, then drop out for a few more 
> minutes, then be ok for an hour, etc.

Is there a microwave oven in the path somewhere? That'll play havoc 
with certain wireless frequencies.

--Paul Heinlein <heinlein at madboa.com>

From ali at axian.com  Tue Feb 15 08:48:03 2005
From: ali at axian.com (Alice Corbin)
Date: Tue, 15 Feb 2005 08:48:03 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] emacs keys in an xterm
In-Reply-To: <20050214211101.GA32407@zaphod.axian.com>
References: <20050214211101.GA32407@zaphod.axian.com>
Message-ID: <20050215164803.GA13331@zaphod.axian.com>

I finally got it working, after finding a HowTo at:

http://tiny-tools.sourceforge.net/emacs-keys.html#case_study_ndash_connection_to

In short, I adding the following to my xterm translation table 
in the X resources on my local box (in my system, in 
/usr/X11R6/lib/X11/app-defaults/XTerm) *before*
the definitions for the unmodified keypad keys:

Ctrl ~Meta ~Shift     <Key> Home:  string(0x1d) string("H") \n\
Ctrl ~Meta ~Shift     <Key> End:   string(0x1d) string("E") \n\
Ctrl ~Meta ~Shift     <Key> Left:  string(0x1d) string("L") \n\
Ctrl ~Meta ~Shift     <Key> Right: string(0x1d) string("R") \n\
Ctrl ~Meta ~Shift     <Key> Up:    string(0x1d) string("U") \n\
Ctrl ~Meta ~Shift     <Key> Down:  string(0x1d) string("D") \n\


And on the remote box I added these key definitions to my .emacs tree:

(define-key global-map (kbd "C-] H") 'beginning-of-buffer)
(define-key global-map (kbd "C-] E") 'end-of-buffer)
(define-key global-map (kbd "C-] L") 'backward-word)
(define-key global-map (kbd "C-] R") 'forward-word)
(define-key global-map (kbd "C-] U") 'backward-paragraph)
(define-key global-map (kbd "C-] D") 'forward-paragraph)

Ali

David Fleck wrote:
> We've got a small network set up in our house.  
> 
> I'd like to fix this situation, if possible.  I don't think the router can 
> be moved, but I wonder if another router might have greater signal 
> strength, and if that might solve the problem.  The current router is a 
> Netgear MR814v2 - we used to have Linksys but gave up on them because of a 
> strange, difficult-to-track-down incompatibility with FreeBSD 
> (apparently).


Others have identified the two common interference devices 2.4ghz phones and 
microwave ovens.  Rather than using another router consider a different
antenna on the one you have.  Stock antennas are pretty low grade.

You might also post this question to the folks at Personal Telco Project - 
lots of experience there.

On Fri, Feb 11, 2005 at 07:55:53PM -0800, Matthew Van Gordon wrote:
> Everyone, thanks for your replies.
> 
> I ended up just configuring a BIND9 server to only publish info for it's 
> authoratative domain.
> 
> I'd still like to know why the djbdns-installer for debian made it so 
> that ns and mxz records had their respective type inserted into their 
> FQDNs.
> 
> Be well,.

I've been using dnscache/tinydns since 1999, and continue to do so and
deploy it for clients.  Rsync+ssh is much better for zone transfers, and
I like the separation of authorative server vs. caching.  I may go back
to BIND just to keep up to date with the latest configurations.  I don't
think djbdns will be implementing DNSsec anytime soon which may get
adopted (but has been stalled greatly the last few years).

For reference, here is my config for subtend.net:

# SUBTEND.NET

.subtend.net::navi.subtend.net:
.subtend.net::lumen.cirka.net:

.208.78.64.in-addr.arpa::navi.subtend.net:

=navi.subtend.net:216.183.109.14:
+subtend.net:64.146.134.233:
+www.subtend.net:64.146.134.233:
+ns1.subtend.net:64.146.134.233:

From pca at hevanet.com  Tue Feb 15 11:01:56 2005
From: pca at hevanet.com (Phillip Anderson)

Subject: [PLUG] Home wireless network dropouts
References: <20050215063534.Y94432@grond.sourballs.org><Pine.OSX.4.62.0502150718270.5472@arrowhead.galois.com><20050215163456.GB18545@rauhaus.org>
	<ca51655305021508447a264654@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <000801c51390$d230d080$0202a8c0@LAPTOP>

Any retail wireless router is not going to have much signal strength they 
put in small transmitters to help protect the home user network any cordless 
phone running the 2.4 GHz is going to have a better transmitter then the 
router he with the biggest transmitter wins in this case you may try 
switching it the A band instead of the b or g. A works on 5Mhz and will 
solve the phone problem you can buy a second router and set it up as a 
wireless repeater an access point might also work, or you can drop a grand 
on a Cisco or foundry product which will broadcast throughout your 
neighborhood

Maodhog,

What is your setting for flow control (hardware/software)?  Both should be set
to "no" with the 2500 series.

Also, keep in mind that the 2500 series is somewhat venerable and takes awhile
to boot if you are using a more recent version of IOS (which would be anything
in the 12.x series =) 

It certainly looks like you aren't stuck in rommon, so the only thing I would
suggest is either patience or checking your flow control settings (disable both
hardware and software) in your console proggy.

Regards,
Max

Michael Rasmussen wrote:

>David Fleck wrote:
>  
>
>>We've got a small network set up in our house.  
>>
>>I'd like to fix this situation, if possible.  I don't think the router can 
>>be moved, but I wonder if another router might have greater signal 
>>strength, and if that might solve the problem.  The current router is a 
>>Netgear MR814v2 - we used to have Linksys but gave up on them because of a 
>>strange, difficult-to-track-down incompatibility with FreeBSD 
>>(apparently).
>>    
>>
Another possible source of interference is flourescent lights.  I had
one once that completely blocked a Linksys 802.11b ap on channel 6.
Moving it to channel 10 (?) fixed it.

I've also had audible interference on a 2.4gig cordless phone from a
wireless ap; if there was any active traffic on wireless there would be
a lot of static and short dropouts.  I finally decided that if I was going
to use cordless phones at all, they should be in a different band.  If
you're using a 2.4gig wireless system, use 900mhz or 5gig cordless
phones.  If you need 2.4ghz phones, use 802.11a (5ghz) wireless.

You can try higher gain or directional antennas to get from one side
of the house to the other.

dgc

Quantian is a re-mastered version of Knoppix (3.6)
that emphasizes scientific applications. The most
current version (0.6.9.3) is about 2 GB and runs from
a DVD. I am using this DVD right now.

I suppose that, like Knoppix, it can be installed on a
hard drive. Although it has tons of applications, it
is conceivable that a user might want more. I have
heard others complain that when Debian is installed
from Knoppix, the use of apt-get may be difficult to
configure, and the version of sources.list on the DVD
isn't exactly to my liking. [I wouldn't expect apt-get
to work at all from the CD, since it is now impossible
to install anything system-wide.] Would anyone who has
installed Knoppix on a hard drive like to comment on
this point?

From znmeb at cesmail.net  Tue Feb 15 22:58:06 2005
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Tue, 15 Feb 2005 22:58:06 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Quantian
In-Reply-To: <20050216044322.83891.qmail@web50604.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20050216044322.83891.qmail@web50604.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <4212EEFE.7010409@cesmail.net>

Robert Kopp wrote:

>Quantian is a re-mastered version of Knoppix (3.6)
>that emphasizes scientific applications. The most
>current version (0.6.9.3) is about 2 GB and runs from
>a DVD. I am using this DVD right now.
>
>I suppose that, like Knoppix, it can be installed on a
>hard drive. Although it has tons of applications, it
>is conceivable that a user might want more. I have
>heard others complain that when Debian is installed
>from Knoppix, the use of apt-get may be difficult to
>configure, and the version of sources.list on the DVD
>isn't exactly to my liking. [I wouldn't expect apt-get
>to work at all from the CD, since it is now impossible
>to install anything system-wide.] Would anyone who has
>installed Knoppix on a hard drive like to comment on
>this point?
>

I haven't done this recently, but there are a few things you do have to 
watch out for. First of all, when you run the hard disk install script, 
bail out before letting it install, saving your config file. Then go in 
and edit the config file to your liking and restart the installer, using 
the edited config file.

Second, although reiserfs is a **wonderful** filesystem, if you plan to 
move partitions around with "parted" or "qtparted", don't use anything 
but ext2 or ext3 partitions. There aren't reliable utilities available 
for changing sizes or moving reiserfs partitions yet.

Third, after you get the hard disk install done, you will need to edit 
sources.list.

Now the really tricky part. Knoppix, clusterKnoppix and Quantian all 
have a number of packages that deviate from the standard Debian 
repositories. If you just blindly do an apt-get upgrade or try to 
install packages, you'll end up deleting stuff that you need. I never 
really found an elegant way around this, and gave up on hard disk 
installs of Knoppix and its derivatives.

It's almost as fast, assuming broadband, to do a "sarge" install from 
scratch over the Internet, and install all the packages on Quantian, or 
some subset thereof, or some superset thereof. There's actually a list 
somewhere on the Quantian page (http://dirk.eddelbuettel.com/quantian) 
of all the packages that he adds, and it would be very easy to make an 
"apt-get" script from this list.

If you do want to try and work around this, there is a very inelegant 
hack I found. Bring up "dselect". Go into the "Select" menu and "hold" 
all the packages! The easiest way to do this is to sort by section only, 
go to the top, and hit an equal sign ("="). It will give you a fairly 
long list of packages, which you can hold by holding down the "=" key 
till it stops at the bottom. Then hit "Return", and you should be at the 
very bottom of the package list, and all packages will be held -- they 
will stay in the state they're in.

Now you can go ahead and add the packages you want to add. But do it in 
small steps, and watch carefully. "apt-get install" will list all the 
actions it's about to take, and if you see it wanting to delete 
important stuff, when it asks you if you want to do the install, say 
"No". Then go back into "dselect" and hold ("=") everything, and start over.

Bah! That's one of the reasons I went to Gentoo :).

Don't change any jumper settings, unless you have the manual.  You need
to figure out if the router has really stopped booting, or if you have
just lost communication with it.  It looks to me like it stopped
communicating near the beginning of loading the configuration, where the
baud rate of the console is defined, if that rate is different from the
default baud rate, you will have to tell Minicom (dump hyper-terminal it
sucks) about it; fortunately there aren't that many choices (and you
don't have to reboot your computer after each change).

Good Luck,

Derek Loree

From aaronb at personaltelco.net  Wed Feb 16 09:50:31 2005
From: aaronb at personaltelco.net (Aaron Baer)
Date: Wed, 16 Feb 2005 09:50:31 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Home wireless network dropouts
In-Reply-To: <4212B297.30707@easystreet.com>
References: <20050215063534.Y94432@grond.sourballs.org>
	<20050215185047.GB25852@patch.com>  <4212B297.30707@easystreet.com>
Message-ID: <1108576232.5136.29.camel@localhost.localdomain>

On Tue, 2005-02-15 at 18:40 -0800, Dave Clemans wrote:
> Another possible source of interference is flourescent lights.  I had
> one once that completely blocked a Linksys 802.11b ap on channel 6.
> Moving it to channel 10 (?) fixed it.

Keep in mind that in the 802.11b/g frequency allocation for the US there
are only three NON-overlapping channels. 1 - 6 - 11. All other channels
will bleed over into the neighboring channels causing interference
especially in a dense wireless area.

Have you had a chance to run something like Kismet to find out if there
are other wireless networks in the area. It could be that you're in the
path of 2 low signals and your client card is having a hard time
figuring out which to use. 

Make sure that you have a distinct essid set from any other network that
is not part of your wireless network and that you are on a channel 1,6
or 11 that is not the same channel as any other network in the area.

You do have options with different types of antennas and remember that
your current AP has, at most, a 3dBi omni on it by default. If that AP
is on the complete opposite side of the house and you're on the edge of
the signal "donut" that it creates something you might consider is
moving the device as close to the center of your house as possible.
Otherwise, your sending half of the signal coverage right out of the
side of your house opposite of where you are trying to use it. Adding a
5, 7, or 9dBi omni will of course add to the coverage but you may then
be broadcasting your network further than you really want to. (However,
I highly recommend building wireless networks in your community as you
might expect).

Remember it's not how loud you broadcast your signal outward it's more
about how sensitively your network is able to "listen" to the radio
signals that are trying to use it. 

A-
-- 
----
Aaron Baer
aaronb at personaltelco.net
http://www.personaltelco.net/
http://aaron.slyness.org/

From AthlonRob at axpr.net  Wed Feb 16 17:14:20 2005
From: AthlonRob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)

On Wed, 2005-02-16 at 17:05 -0800, Evan Heidtmann wrote:
> Do both exim and postfix deliver mail to the same location by default? 

Where does exim drop it?  Postfix uses /var/spool/mail/$USER by default,
using mbox.  That can easily be configured to go elsewhere or use
Maildir.

> Is there anything else I should be aware of or look out for?

Postfix really is pretty straightforward to set up and get going... 'tis
almost idiot proof, although I *have* managed to mess things up a few
times.  ;-)

From ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net  Wed Feb 16 20:36:14 2005
From: ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Wed, 16 Feb 2005 22:36:14 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] ssh pass phrase authorization
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0502161813460.25159@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0502161813460.25159@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <200502162236.14299.ewilhelm@sbcglobal.net>

# The following was supposedly scribed by
# Rich Shepard
# on Wednesday 16 February 2005 08:18 pm:

>? ?Somehow, I have managed to lose pass phrase authentification when I
> ssh from one host to another, either here in the office or from the
> portable when I'm away from the office. Used to work; don't know why
> it stopped.

You mean it is asking for the pass phrase for your private key?

Your public key is in the ~/.ssh/authorized_keys file on the remote 
host?

Your ~/.ssh/authorized_keys on the remote host has the correct (600) 
permissions?

You are running ssh-agent and did ssh-add "privatekeyfile" or you have a 
key in ~/.ssh/identity (mine is a symlink to my favorite private key) 
or you are using the -i "privatekeyfile" option?

What error are you getting (or is it just prompting you for a pasword?)

If it stopped using your key and the filename wasn't ~/.ssh/identity, it 
might be related to an X/wm upgrade (different distros seem to have 
different policies about that.)  I seem to remember something like that 
a while back where debian would automatically use ~/.ssh/id_dsa with 
ssh-agent on startx or something and (then quit?) details are fuzzy.

--Eric

On 2005-02-17, Evan Heidtmann <clydefrog at adnap.no-ip.com> wrote:

>> > Do both exim and postfix deliver mail to the same location by default?

>> Where does exim drop it?  Postfix uses /var/spool/mail/$USER by default,
>> using mbox.  That can easily be configured to go elsewhere or use
>> Maildir.
>
> That's where exim puts it, sort of. On my system, /var/spool/mail is a
> symlink to /var/mail, which contains the mboxes.

/var/spool/mail is the FHS-canonical location for such things; /var/mail
should be a symlink to that, rather than the other way around.  Of
course, if you're using Debian it's probably based on 10 year-old
conventions.

From merlyn at stonehenge.com  Thu Feb 17 06:28:04 2005
From: merlyn at stonehenge.com (Randal L. Schwartz)
Date: 17 Feb 2005 06:28:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Meta: how not to announce (was Re: LinuxFest NW Volunteers
	Needed)
References: <1108521170.19505.117.camel@localhost>
	<20050216105226.591d1590.jason@vancleve.com>
Message-ID: <86psyz1csr.fsf_-_@blue.stonehenge.com>

>>>>> "Jason" == Jason Van Cleve <jason at vancleve.com> writes:

Jason> Quoth D. Cooper Stevenson, on Tue, 15 Feb 2005 18:32:50 -0800:
>> So if you're going to the LFNW this year and would like to help out,

Jason> Where is it held?  Is there a link?

And since everyone had that same question, let this be a perfect
example of how *not* to announce something. :)

Sure, the dates, hours, address, phone numbers, and URL of your
pet project may be emblazoned into your skull (or bookmarklist), but
if you're asking others for money, time, or "pass it on", please
include all relevant details in your announcement.

*Especially* define random acronyms or terms.  Not everyone has the
same background.  However, I don't think you need to explain the word
"Linux" on this list, for example.

On Wed, 16 Feb 2005, Eric Wilhelm wrote:

> You mean it is asking for the pass phrase for your private key?

Eric,

   No, I mean that I am _not_ asked for my passphrase but only for my
password.

> Your public key is in the ~/.ssh/authorized_keys file on the remote
> host?

   Yes. My  public keys for all hosts are in each host's
~/.ssh/authorized_keys.

> Your ~/.ssh/authorized_keys on the remote host has the correct (600)

From ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net  Thu Feb 17 07:57:14 2005
From: ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Thu, 17 Feb 2005 09:57:14 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] ssh pass phrase authorization
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0502170725410.5000@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0502161813460.25159@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<200502162236.14299.ewilhelm@sbcglobal.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0502170725410.5000@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <200502170957.14478.ewilhelm@sbcglobal.net>

# The following was supposedly scribed by
# Rich Shepard
# on Thursday 17 February 2005 09:35 am:

>In ~/.ssh, identity/identity_pub have a timestamp of 2000-02-18 while
>id_dsa/id_dsa.pub have a timestamp of 2003-12-06.

I guess .ssh/identity is for version 1 and id_dsa is for version 2.

Are you using the '-i' option?

Try 'ssh-agent bash', then do 'ssh-add .ssh/id_dsa'.  That should force 
the use of that key.  If you still get a password prompt when you try 
to ssh from inside of that shell, it means your public key isn't right 
on the other end (IIRC.)

>? ?My understanding of the process comes from reading Mick Bauer's
> book and I know that the password is passed through the encrypted
> tunnel whether I'm here or away from the office. However, using my
> passphrase when I'm away from the office adds that modicum of
> additional security and that's why I'd like to resolve this issue.

If you have password logins enabled on the server, then using a key 
isn't really more secure, just more convenient if it's a passwordless 
key.  The security advantage of using keys comes when you configure the 
server to disallow password logins, so that only the holder of the 
private key (who also holds the passphrase for that key) can login.  
This prevents the social-engineering/guess-my-mother's-maiden-name type 
of attack.

If you really want to add security when you are away from the office, 
configure the office machine with two ssh servers.  One running on port 
22 and allowing password logins from all users, the other running on 
port 26 (or something) with (optionally) only a select list of users 
allowed to login with only dsa keys.  Then get the firewall to forward 
port 22 connections to port 26 on that box.  There are some other 
tricks involved, so let me know if you're interested.

--Eric

It's in there, if you have an up-to-date Gimp-print. I have version 4.2.7 and

Direct-connect at least lets you test that the driver works. Bump the logging 
level up to 'debug' and see what you find in /var/log/cups/error_log.

their table of Epson printers, the R300 works, but not every feature is 
supported.

> Since the board powered up and continued to function as a hub(it would 
> pass traffic fro computer to printer)then it appeared to either be a

It's not unheard of for the console port on the router itself to be bad.
It looks as though the router is booting up properly, and sending output
to the console, but not responding to input on the console.

You also could have a bad console cable, or one with an incorrect
pinout.

--Stafford

Found it! At least with perl regex's:

/^\!Image: (?!Compiled)/

Matches this:
!Image: slot0:c6sup12-pk2sv-mz.121-23.E2.bin

But not this:
!Image: Compiled: Sat 30-Oct-04 21:45 by yiyan

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Thu Feb 17 13:19:10 2005
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Thu, 17 Feb 2005 13:19:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] ssh pass phrase authorization
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0502171301100.7340@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0502161813460.25159@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0502161945390.30823@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<4214F7C4.5050104@easystreet.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0502171301100.7340@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <6691-58177@sneakemail.com>

On Thu, 17 Feb 2005 13:03:37 -0800 (PST), Rich Shepard wrote:

>    Looking at this, it seems that the originating host sent a public key, but
> the attempt failed to be completed. Is this correct?

That's correct.  For security reasons, ssh doesn't tell the client the
specific reason why it rejected the public key.  This is only
available in the sshd server logs, and even then I think you have to
have the debug level turned up.

What I usually check:
  + proper key in authorized_keys
  + no line-feeds in authorized_keys on the same key
  + permissions on authorized_keys
  + permissions on .ssh
  + permissions on your home directory

Permissions that are too generous can cause it to fail.  For example,
a group-writeable home directory breaks ssh public key authentication
to protect you from yourself.

If all the above have failed, as root on one of the servers run sshd
in debug mode on some wierd port.  Note that you may need to open this
port in iptables/ipchains first:

# /usr/sbin/sshd -ddd -p 2222

>From your host connect via:

$ ssh -v -p 2222 <host where the above is running>

The "-ddd" debug level should provide some help on finding the problem.

  -- Steve

On Thu, 17 Feb 2005 13:16:30 -0800 (PST), Rich Shepard wrote:

>    Seeing the output of the verbose exchange caused me to look closely at
> what I have going here. On salmo:~/.ssh/known_hosts have keys beginning
> "ssh-rsa" while authorized_keys has "ssh-dss". Looks like a mis-match
> between RSA and DSA. But, that directory also has the private and public key
> files id_dsa and id_dsa.pub.

The known_hosts keys are used to identify the servers-- not users. 
This shouldn't affect your ability to log in with public keys unless
one of the host keys is incorrect, in which case ssh should give you a
big ugly warning about a bad host key.

  -- Steve

Brute force?

#include <math.h>
int x, y;

y = floor(log10((double)x)) + 1;


or 

int x, y;

y = 1;
while (x /= 10)
  y++;


Untested, of course.

From shahms at shahms.com  Thu Feb 17 14:07:03 2005
From: shahms at shahms.com (Shahms King)
Date: Thu, 17 Feb 2005 14:07:03 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] OT:  Formula For Determining # Of Digits In An Integer
In-Reply-To: <20050217135504.5a85589e.jason@vancleve.com>
References: <20050217135504.5a85589e.jason@vancleve.com>
Message-ID: <1108678023.9480.8.camel@shahms.mesd.k12.or.us>

Yeah, assuming you want "base 10 digits", the function you're looking
for is log.  Assuming you're using the Standard C library you want:

int digits = (int)(log(n)/log(10)) + 1

log(n) -> the natural log of a number
log(n)/log(base) -> the logarithm of n in "base" (10 in this case)

n = base^x, log(n, base) -> x

So, a given number 'n', is representable as 1.<something> x 10^l.
You don't care about the <something> all you want is the "10^l" part, to
get the digits (minus the ones place).  Obviously log will give you a
double, but you don't care about fractional digits.

Yeah, in my mail (buried in with the rest of the stuff) is the simple
formula for computing a log in an arbitrary base:

log(n, base) -> log(n)/log(base)

On Thu, 17 Feb 2005 14:21:59 -0800 (PST), Rich Shepard wrote:


>    Using 'ssh -2' I'm now asked for my passphrase from salmo to tux, but not
> the other direction. Sigh. :-)
> 
>    On both hosts, authorized_keys2 have the id_dsa.pub of the other hosts,
> but not themselves.

This sounds correct.  I usually use authorized_keys (not
authorized_keys2) but OpenSSH reads both files.

>    It doesn't seem to matter if the public key is in known_hosts or not.

This is correct.  known_hosts is only used to validate that a host is
"known" and there isn't someone intercepting your TCP streams and
doing nasty things to it.  It has absolutely no bearing whatsoever on
public key vs. password authentication.  I suggest that you not worry
about this file at all for now.

> I cannot determine if that's a ssh-1 or rsa feature not used in ssh-2 and dsa.
> My google search (+ssh +known_hosts) doesn't reveal if this file is specific
> to a ssh version or encryption method.

Don't sweat this file.  It doesn't affect logging in via key.
 
>    This evening I'll fire up the portables and try them. In the meantime I
> need to get some work done.

Bah!  Work.

This ssh connection debugging document might be helpful:

http://cfm.gs.washington.edu/security/ssh/client-pkauth/problems/

From ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net  Thu Feb 17 15:11:01 2005
From: ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Thu, 17 Feb 2005 17:11:01 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] OT: Formula For Determining # Of Digits In An Integer

Message-ID: <200502171711.01531.ewilhelm@sbcglobal.net>

# The following was supposedly scribed by
# Jason Van Cleve
# on Thursday 17 February 2005 04:31 pm:

>> y = 1;
>> while (x /= 10)
>> ? y++;
>
>This is basically what I have now. ?It's just that the method I'm
>writing is already called in a loop, so I wanted a single-operation
>formula instead of a loop algorithm.

Long version of Galens reply:
You might want to benchmark the loop vs the log() method.  I'm not sure 
exactly how java does it or whether it's somehow native in the 
processor, but IIRC from mathematics, logarithms are only computable as 
an infinite series.  So, barring some magic happening in the processor 
that I don't know about, a hand-written log() function (which iterates 
until the desired accuracy is reached (and remember, you're going to 
call this twice)) is going to be slower than your simple reductive 
loop.

Ok, so none of the details are too sharp here.  Point is:  benchmark.  
Don't assume that mathematical elegance is always faster than brute 
force.

Anyway, I'd probably just do length(sprintf("%0.0f", $num)) in perl 
(assuming a positive number.)

--Eric

>>>>> "glen" == glen e p ropella <gepr at acm.org> writes:

jvc> In any case, I wanted to know the formula (and I got it in

glen> (defun numdig (n) (if (< n 10) 1 (+ 1 (numdig (/ n 10)))))

Heh.  I *had* fired up CLISP (the ideal desktop calculator) to
investigate.

And folks, the beautiful thing about Lisp here is that the division
there at the end results in a rational number, not a float and not
just a truncated integer like in C.  Inconsequently perhaps, but
beautiful nevertheless.  

These days a hard-core common lisper might scowl at the recursion as
scheme-ish and use LOOP instead[1], but I find yours adroitly elegant.

[1] par example (ugh!):

  (defun numdig2 (n) 
    (loop for digit from 1 
          for val = n then (/ val 10) 
          when (< val 10) do (return digit)))

However...if you cast the division result to a float you will always get 
the correct answer.

for (int n = 1; n < 100000000; n = n * 10) {
            double log = (float)(Math.log(n) / Math.log(10));
            System.out.println("Log10 of " + n + " is " + log);
 }

Eric


From travis_brooks at hotmail.com  Fri Feb 18 00:30:18 2005
From: travis_brooks at hotmail.com (Travis Brooks)
Date: Fri, 18 Feb 2005 08:30:18 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] RE: [PJUG Javamail] Re-resolved: OT: Formula For Determining
	# Of Digits . . .
In-Reply-To: <20050217205058.58c1131f.jason@vancleve.com>
Message-ID: <BAY101-F402C9EAC4F7055784A66D7936E0@phx.gbl>

I asked this question a few years back, and i too got the "use log" answer, 
and proceeded with the standard "d'oh!  why didn't i think of that!".  
Besides accuracy the other thing to consider is performance.  I just did a 
timing test on my machine using an array of 1 million random integers and 
was a little surprised to see that converting to string and getting the 
length took about 30% less time than the numerical log route.  But by far 
the *fastest* method i could think up was this one (which is around 10x 
faster than the string method):

    public int intLen(int val){
        //max =     2147483648 (note, 1 greater than maximum int)
        //10          1000000000
        //9            100000000
        //8            10000000
        //7            1000000
        //6            100000
        //5            10000
        //4            1000
        //3            100
        //2            10
        //1            1
        long absVal = (long)Math.abs((long)val);
        if(absVal >= 1000000000)
            return 10;
        if(absVal >= 100000000)
            return 9;
        if(absVal >= 10000000)
            return 8;
        if(absVal >= 1000000)
            return 7;
        if(absVal >= 100000)
            return 6;
        if(absVal >= 10000)
            return 5;
        if(absVal >= 1000)
            return 4;
        if(absVal >= 100)
            return 3;
        if(absVal >= 10)
            return 2;
        return 1;
    }


The conversion to long is needed for the outlier case of the minimum integer 
(which should be done if you're converting to string too).  Not what i'd 
call pretty, but it flies!

-travis



Give a man a fish and he eats a fish, but teach a man to fish at rock-bottom 
wages and we all shall eat his fish.

From jshirah at attglobal.net  Fri Feb 18 02:23:01 2005
From: jshirah at attglobal.net (Joe Sam Shirah)
Date: Fri, 18 Feb 2005 05:23:01 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] Re: [PJUG Javamail] Re-resolved: OT: Formula For Determining
	# Of Digits . . .
References: <BAY101-F402C9EAC4F7055784A66D7936E0@phx.gbl>
Message-ID: <034e01c515a4$5ce3a5d0$3b4e410c@savaj001>


    Hi Travis,

> But by far the *fastest* method i could think up was this one...

    My thoughts exactly.  I hadn't posted before because I was going to do
the timing tests first, but I'm happy to take your word for it.  Following
is my version, which should be faster overall, assuming a random
distribution of input values.

-----------------------------------

public class IntLength
{

  public int calcLength( int in )
  {
    if( in == Integer.MIN_VALUE ) { return 10; }

    if( in < 0 ) { in = -in; } // to positive value

    if( in > 99999 )
    {
      if( in > 999999999 ) { return 10; }
      if( in > 99999999 )  { return 9; }
      if( in > 9999999 )   { return 8; }
      if( in > 999999 )    { return 7; }
      return 6;
    }
    else
    {
      if( in > 9999 ) { return 5; }
      if( in > 999 )  { return 4; }
      if( in > 99 )   { return 3; }
      if( in > 9 )    { return 2; }
      return 1;
    }
  } // end calcLength


  public static void main( String[] args )
  {
    IntLength il = new IntLength();

    for( int n = 1; n < 100000000; n *= 10 )
    {
      System.out.println( "n: " + n +
        "; length: " + il.calcLength( n ) );
    }

    System.out.println();

    for( int n = -1; n > -100000000; n *= 10 )
    {
      System.out.println( "n: " + n +
        "; length: " + il.calcLength( n ) );
    }

    System.out.println(  );
    System.out.println( "min: " + Integer.MIN_VALUE +
      "; length: " + il.calcLength( Integer.MIN_VALUE ) );
    System.out.println( "max: " + Integer.MAX_VALUE +
      "; length: " + il.calcLength( Integer.MAX_VALUE ) );

  } // end main

} // end class IntLength

-----------------------------------

    I'm sure it could be made faster, but this is probably Good Enough ( as
is yours ) for most purposes.

--- Joe Sam Shirah <jshirah at attglobal.net> wrote:

> 
>     Hi Travis,
> 
> > But by far the *fastest* method i could think up was this one...
> 
>     My thoughts exactly.  I hadn't posted before because I was
> going to do the timing tests first, but I'm happy to take your
> word for it.  Following is my version, which should be faster
> overall, assuming a random distribution of input values.

It will depend on the distribution or your data, but if the data is
random, Travis's method will be more efficient.

Keep in mind that about 53% of random 32 bit ints are 10 digits.  Of
the remaining values 90% are 9 digits, 9% are 8 digits, .9% are 7
digits, etc.  

You don't get much leverage by checking whether a number is > 99999,
because like 99.995% are.
 
Jon

Jon LeVitre said:
> It will depend on the distribution or your data, but if the data is
> random, Travis's method will be more efficient.

Ok, if we're going to really get geeky here,  :-) let's consider space as
well as time efficiency:

Another problem with the easy string-based solution could be object
creation.  The OP's original problem mentioned a number like '032', so it
sounds like it's a string.  So the operation would be;

   String -> Integer -> String -> Compute length

Two new objects would be created per execution.

By the way, JDK 1.5 has a new method, Math.log10() ... which doesn't
suffer from the rounding error.

Robb


From jshirah at attglobal.net  Fri Feb 18 12:27:02 2005
From: jshirah at attglobal.net (Joe Sam Shirah)
Date: Fri, 18 Feb 2005 15:27:02 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] Re: [PJUG Javamail] Re-resolved: OT: Formula For Determining
	# Of Digits . . .
References: <20050218190718.57381.qmail@web52710.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <017e01c515f8$395fa790$e83d410c@savaj001>


    Hi Jon,

    Sorry about being loose with words.  I didn't mean technical random
distributions.  My real thought was applications in general rather than,
say, a NASA app that deals with intergalactic distances.  I wouldn't argue
with anyone about it, but I suspect that most ( particualrly business ) apps
don't see 53% of their integer values at 10 digits.

    My selection of > 99999 was purely arbitrary in a binary way.  Again, I
wouldn't argue if someone decided there was a dividing point more optimal
for most applications.

    What I would say, is that the cost of these few statements:

    if( in == Integer.MIN_VALUE ) { return 10; }

    if( in < 0 ) { in = -in; } // to positive value

    if( in > 99999 )

is most likely less than the method call to Math.abs(), the casts and long
comparisons in the previous code.  Using one compare to potentially save 4
compares seemed like a reasonable trade-off to me, especially at this point.
I suspect further optimizations would require a huge number of invocations
to even register.  But I'll be happy to be proven wrong if you would like to
supply the data.

    Let me be clear here: I'm not attacking Travis' code at all.  It's clear
that he posted first and saw that Java's strict definition of type sizes
allowed a solution that is "better" - depending, as always on your
definition of better - than the correct or proper classical solution.  I
just wanted to clarify since you brought up the point.

    I should also mention that I don't advocate "magic" numbers in general;
this is just a special case that Java specifications make valid and is
probably one of the "exceptions that prove the rule."

Speaking of efficieny, how efficient is Math.log10() ? How about 

x = the number itself
int count = 0;
while (x >= 1) {
 x = x/10;
 count++;
}
return count;




On Fri, 2005-02-18 at 11:27, Robb Shecter wrote:
> Jon LeVitre said:
> > It will depend on the distribution or your data, but if the data is
> > random, Travis's method will be more efficient.
> 
> Ok, if we're going to really get geeky here,  :-) let's consider space as
> well as time efficiency:
> 
> Another problem with the easy string-based solution could be object
> creation.  The OP's original problem mentioned a number like '032', so it
> sounds like it's a string.  So the operation would be;
> 
>    String -> Integer -> String -> Compute length
> 
> Two new objects would be created per execution.
> 
> By the way, JDK 1.5 has a new method, Math.log10() ... which doesn't
> suffer from the rounding error.
>

patches to Mailman that add a search function;  has anyone
here tried them?  If they work, I would like to try them on
my mailing lists, and maybe we should add them to the PLUG

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Sat Feb 19 00:07:35 2005
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Sat, 19 Feb 2005 00:07:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] RealTek 8180 driver seg faults...
In-Reply-To: <20050218202552.AA7851F5AC@robinson-west.com>
References: <20050218202552.AA7851F5AC@robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <200502190007.36474.carla@bratgrrl.com>

On Friday 18 February 2005 12:25 pm, robinsom at robinson-west.com wrote:
> Feb 18 13:28:04 condor kernel: 
> Feb 18 13:28:04 condor kernel: 
> Feb 18 13:28:04 condor kernel: ******** RTL8180 Wireless Lan Driver 
2003-11-24 loaded********
> Feb 18 13:28:04 condor kernel: PCI: Assigned IRQ 5 for device 00:0a.0

Are you sure this is right driver for your wireless adapter?
Do you know what your chipset is?
RTL8180 chipsets don't work well in Linux. Part of the driver is a closed 
binary, and even on Windows RealTek tend to suck. But I have heard of success 
using Ndiswrapper. This is a good howto:
http://www.linuxquestions.org/questions/showthread.php?postid=877345#post877345

For trouble-free, reliable operation I always use the Senao/Engenius 2511.  
It's 802.11b, has about seven times more transmit power than other wireless 
adapters, and it works great in Linux. It also makes a nice homegrown access 
point.

Rant: Wireless adapter vendors play all kinds of games with chipsets. They 
change them frequently and without disclosure. And even when you think you 
have a chipset that is supported in Linux, many times the radio chip is good 
but the firmware is some weird proprietary piece of Windoze-only junk. Some 
of them even deliberately obscure the chipset IDs, so 'cardctl ident' or 
'lscpi' don't help. This tells you how to identify chipsets with the FCC 
registry:
http://www.linuxplanet.com/linuxplanet/tutorials/5736/5/
They're not supposed to lie to the FCC, so this tells all.

From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Sat Feb 19 09:30:29 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 19 Feb 2005 09:30:29 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] USB card reader - how to read from all 6 slots
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0502190908290.5935@localhost>

Sorry I wasn't a member of this mailing list soon enough to get this
posting attached to the USB card reader thread.  I wanted to share a bit
of info anyway:

My card reader shows up as a SCSI device with multiple LUNs.  In order
to get the kernel to probe all the LUNs, thereby making each slot
appear as a separate SCSI sevice, I needed to enable an option in the
kernel config:

Network drivers > SCSI device support > Probe all LUNs on each SCSI
device

This is in the 2.6 kernel.  Perhaps it's in the 2.4 kernel too - I can't
say for sure - I don't have a 2.4 kernel handy.  Once I enabled this
option, I see in dmesg:

Vendor: Y-E DATA  Model: CF Card Reader    Rev: 1.03
Type:   Direct-Access                      ANSI SCSI revision: 02
Vendor: Y-E DATA  Model: SM Card Reader    Rev: 1.03
Type:   Direct-Access                      ANSI SCSI revision: 02
Vendor: Y-E DATA  Model: MS Card Reader    Rev: 1.03
Type:   Direct-Access                      ANSI SCSI revision: 02
Attached scsi removable disk sda at scsi0, channel 0, id 0, lun 0
Attached scsi removable disk sdb at scsi0, channel 0, id 0, lun 1
Attached scsi removable disk sdc at scsi0, channel 0, id 0, lun 2
Vendor: Y-E DATA  Model: SD Card Reader    Rev: 1.03
Type:   Direct-Access                      ANSI SCSI revision: 02
Attached scsi removable disk sdd at scsi0, channel 0, id 0, lun 3

And a few lines later...

Attached scsi generic sg0 at scsi0, channel 0, id 0, lun 0,  type 0
Attached scsi generic sg1 at scsi0, channel 0, id 0, lun 1,  type 0
Attached scsi generic sg2 at scsi0, channel 0, id 0, lun 2,  type 0
Attached scsi generic sg3 at scsi0, channel 0, id 0, lun 3,  type 0

4 slots == 4 device nodes.  You're not seeing CDROMs here - these are
truly the 4 slots of my card reader, and clearly the happy result of
this one kernel config option.

From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Sat Feb 19 19:52:04 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sat, 19 Feb 2005 19:52:04 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] PC/XT keyboards and the 2.6 kernel driver
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0502191941210.6581@localhost>

The linux 2.6 kernel has a driver for XT keyboards.  Here's the help for
the config option in menuconfig:

CONFIG_KEYBOARD_XTKBD:

Say Y here if you want to use the old IBM PC/XT keyboard (or
compatible) on your system. This is only possible with a
parallel port keyboard adapter, you cannot connect it to the
keyboard port on a PC that runs Linux.

To compile this driver as a module, choose M here: the
module will be called xtkbd.

Is it really as easy as stringing a bunch of adapters together until you
have the XT plug converted into a DB-25 connector?  Are there any
success stories out there?  I have scoured google for info, but I find
nothing.

This isn't an urgent call for help.  I'll just go ahead and get a few
adapters and see what happens.  I'll post a followup to this so that
there will be at least one meager source of info for others who want to
take their beautiful, 20-pound metal clackers and make them live once
more.

From ckonstan at lunarlogic.com  Sun Feb 20 12:00:09 2005
From: ckonstan at lunarlogic.com (Carlos Konstanski)

Subject: [PLUG] PC/XT keyboards and the 2.6 kernel driver
In-Reply-To: <20050220170308.AF51D8F13@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
References: <20050220170308.AF51D8F13@tinman.seitzassoc.com>

It apears I found another copy of the website that contains the schematic
diagram that is missing from the page that was found by the guy in the
groups-beta.google.com post:

http://atrey.karlin.mff.cuni.cz/~vojtech/input/adapters.html

This is aimed at SUN keyboards.  I'm not a hardware geek, so I don't
know yet if this applies to then IBM XT keyboard.  I think the SUN
keyboard and the IBM XT keyboard have different issues to overcome.  But
the pinout at the DB-25 end is what the kernel driver cares about, and
this is where useful info can be gleaned from this schematic.

One big question is: is the xtkbd driver dependent upon the parkbd
driver, or are they totally independent?  Do they expect the same DB-25
pinout?

There's still confusion, however.  The web page says this circuit works
with the parkbd driver, but the schematic plainly shows a DB-9 connector.
This fight is a long way from being won.  Maybe one is supposed to wire
up a DB-9 connector as shown, and then plug it into a DB-9 to DB-25
adapter.  Who knows.  I do know one guy who knows - Vojtech Vavlik.  If
I can't make headway, I will have no choice but to pester him a bit.  He
should have commented his code.

> Ok, if we're going to really get geeky here,  :-) let's consider space as
> well as time efficiency:
>
> Another problem with the easy string-based solution could be object
> creation.  The OP's original problem mentioned a number like '032', so it
> sounds like it's a string.  So the operation would be;
>
>    String -> Integer -> String -> Compute length
>
> Two new objects would be created per execution.
>

> suffer from the rounding error.

From travis_brooks at hotmail.com  Fri Feb 18 13:43:10 2005
From: travis_brooks at hotmail.com (Travis Brooks)
Date: Fri, 18 Feb 2005 21:43:10 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] Re: [PJUG Javamail] Re-resolved: OT: Formula For Determining
	# Of Digits . . .
In-Reply-To: <20050218190718.57381.qmail@web52710.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <BAY101-F29888A6FCB45AEEB21F6C1936E0@phx.gbl>

well, at the risk of sounding really really geeky, aw what the heck its 
friday...

Joe's method pointed out 2 things that my code was doing that poorly 
effected performance, namely promoting an int to a long and making a method 
call for the absolute value instead of inlining it.  If you change the top 
couple of lines of my original method to:

if(val==Integer.MIN_VALUE)
    return 10;
int absVal = val>0?val:(val * -1);

then it about doubles the speed.  When testing on a random array of 1 
million ints my method came up consistently faster, but for whatever reason 
at 10 million ints Joe's slightly edged mine out.  I wondered if this might 
be an artifact of java.util.Random relying on the crappy old Knuth random 
algorithm, so I did the test again using 
cern.jet.random.engine.MersenneTwister from the Colt library (If you haven't 
used a Mersenne Twister before, check it out!) to fill in the array.  The 
results almost to the millisecond reversed, so Joe's beat my method at 1 
million ints and i came out on top at 10 million.

I think i'm going to call it a tie...and will immediately cease my beating 
of this poor dead horse...

-travis

Give a man a fish and he eats a fish, but teach a man to fish at rock-bottom 
wages and we all shall eat his fish.

From pcmiller at us.ibm.com  Fri Feb 18 12:20:17 2005
From: pcmiller at us.ibm.com (Phillip Miller)
Date: Fri, 18 Feb 2005 12:20:17 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: [PJUG Javamail] Re-resolved: OT: Formula For Determining
 # Of Digits . . .
In-Reply-To: <20050218190718.57381.qmail@web52710.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <OFD6FFF61F.AAFC7E3F-ON88256FAC.006E89C7-88256FAC.006FB706@us.ibm.com>

There are a couple of issues I think are kind of going unnoticed here. 

First of all, when we're talking about a random distribution of integers, 
I think we're perhaps missing an important point: integers in typical user 
programs are almost certainly NOT going to be uniformly distributed from 
-MAXINT to MAXINT; probably for typical programs using typical ranges of 
integers counting typical objects of interest to users, you'd see more of 
a "normal" (bell) probability distribution centered at zero. The larger 
the number, the less likely you'd be to see it (unless you're counting 
atoms).  For this reason, you'd probably find a series of conditionals 
that starts with small numbers to be more efficient in typical programs.

Second, I notice that the solutions using logarithms don't handle boundary 
conditions very well. Almost exactly half the numbers in the integer range 
are negative; you'd want an absolute value operation in the algorithm 
somewhere, or else you'd have runtime exceptions about half the time 
(regardless of the probability distribution). Floating point arithmetic 
solutions introduce a set of exceptional conditions that haven't really 
been accounted for in the algorithms.


-------------------------------------------------
Phil Miller
IT Specialist
IBM Global Services 
Beaverton, Oregon, USA 
ofc: (503)578-5269 voice  /  775-5269 IBM tie line
home: (503)645-4466

--- Joe Sam Shirah <jshirah at attglobal.net> wrote:

> 
>     Hi Travis,
> 
> > But by far the *fastest* method i could think up was this one...
> 
>     My thoughts exactly.  I hadn't posted before because I was
> going to do the timing tests first, but I'm happy to take your
> word for it.  Following is my version, which should be faster
> overall, assuming a random distribution of input values.

It will depend on the distribution or your data, but if the data is
random, Travis's method will be more efficient.

Keep in mind that about 53% of random 32 bit ints are 10 digits.  Of
the remaining values 90% are 9 digits, 9% are 8 digits, .9% are 7
digits, etc. 

You don't get much leverage by checking whether a number is > 99999,
because like 99.995% are.
 
Jon

I suppose technical contacts is the wrong way to describe it. More along the
lines of getting the technical people who are behind pushing Oregon to the
forefront of Open Source. The people who have worked hard over the last five to
ten years, building a culture of open source in the technical community. That
work has paid off in OSDL, the OSU Open Source Lab, FreeGeek, Portland Telco,
and other projects.Those same people, and others, are now working to expand
Open Source beyond merely the technical arena, as exemplified in HB 2892 from a
couple of years ago.

What I envision is helping the technical Open Source advocates get together with
an organization that can provide the political infrastructure to inform and
educate both the public and government as to how Open Source is a benefit to
Oregon economically and socially. Whatever form the help takes, whether passing
legislation, developing policy papers, or just educatiing the public, I think
Open Source advocates could use the politcal contacts to leverage Open Source
for the benefit of Oregon.

Although I am an Open Source advocate, I'm hardly a mover and shaker among the
Open Source community in Oregon. I am a gateway between two worlds though, and
at the moment that's the role I'm attempting to use. In so doing, I'm hoping
someone on this list, or someone on this list knows someone, who is a mover and
shaker within the Open Source community in Oregon and would be willing to sit
down and have a conversation regarding how we could mutually help each other.

Dan H.

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Mon Feb 21 09:05:35 2005
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Mon, 21 Feb 2005 09:05:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] PC/XT keyboards and the 2.6 kernel driver 
In-Reply-To: Your message of "Sun, 20 Feb 2005 12:00:09 PST."
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0502201129190.6181@localhost> 
Message-ID: <20050221170540.3CE708F13@tinman.seitzassoc.com>

Carlos Konstanski <ckonstan at lunarlogic.com> wrote:

> It apears I found another copy of the website that contains the schematic
> diagram that is missing from the page that was found by the guy in the
> groups-beta.google.com post:
> 
> http://atrey.karlin.mff.cuni.cz/~vojtech/input/adapters.html
> 
> This is aimed at SUN keyboards.  I'm not a hardware geek, so I don't
> know yet if this applies to then IBM XT keyboard.  I think the SUN
> keyboard and the IBM XT keyboard have different issues to overcome.  But
> the pinout at the DB-25 end is what the kernel driver cares about, and
> this is where useful info can be gleaned from this schematic.

The Sun keyboard uses a conventional serial interface, so it is different
from the XT keyboard.

> 
> One big question is: is the xtkbd driver dependent upon the parkbd
> driver, or are they totally independent?  Do they expect the same DB-25
> pinout?

I think xtkbd uses parkbd, thus it would use the same pinout.
http://lxr.linux.no/source/drivers/input/serio/Kconfig#L67
http://lxr.linux.no/source/drivers/input/serio/parkbd.c#L191

> 
> There's still confusion, however.  The web page says this circuit works
> with the parkbd driver, but the schematic plainly shows a DB-9 connector.
> This fight is a long way from being won.  Maybe one is supposed to wire
> up a DB-9 connector as shown, and then plug it into a DB-9 to DB-25
> adapter.  Who knows.  I do know one guy who knows - Vojtech Vavlik.  If
> I can't make headway, I will have no choice but to pester him a bit.  He
> should have commented his code.

You should ignore the schematic with the DB9.  You need a schematic that
shows the XT signals connecting to a DB25.

Here's what Vojtech said:
  It's really simple, just two diodes and two resistors in this
  arrangement:
              ______
  +5V -------|______|--.
                       |
  INPUT PIN -----------|
                       |--- KBD PIN
  OUTPUT PIN ---|<|----'

  Now for KBD CLOCK you use STROBE and ACK pins on the parallel port and
  for KBD DATA you use AUTOFD and BUSY pins on the parallel port. I
  don't remember the value of the resistor used, but something like 5k
  should work OK. It's not completely necessary anyway.

  And then you'll also have to connect the keyboard +5V and GND pins. For
  +5V you can either use the real keyboard or mouse connectors, get +5V
  from the joystick connector or from an external power supply. 


Here's the parallel port pinout:
  http://repairfaq.ece.drexel.edu/REPAIR/F_Pinouts2.html#PINOUTS_013
Here's the XT connector pinout:
  http://repairfaq.ece.drexel.edu/REPAIR/F_Pinouts2.html#PINOUTS_017


Suggested diodes would be 1N914, 1N4148, or similar.  Use a 4.7K for
the resistor.  Don't forget that you will need +5V from one of the 
suggested sources.  You can look through the pinout FAQ to find the
connector pinout for whatever source you choose.

galen

(http://netstatus.earthlink.net/netstatus/netstatus.aspx) contains no

like to call "the big bucket of suck."

The only thing that seems remotely related is this:  Occasionally it

doing this when I would start the microwave.  The building I live in is
old and the wiring is poor, so when I start the microwave you can see

microwave is pulling enough power that the DSL modem would be reset.

that's not too terribly expensive?

From ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net  Mon Feb 21 09:48:38 2005
From: ewilhelm at sbcglobal.net (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Mon, 21 Feb 2005 11:48:38 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] I gots me them ol' DSL blues
In-Reply-To: <20050221170432.80324.qmail@web60803.mail.yahoo.com>
